THE Alaá¹…kÄra section in the Agni PurÄá¹‡a is a hopelessly loose heaping of all sorts of ideas taken from this and that writer and does not deserve to be treated seriously as representing any systematic tradition. Dr. De supposes in his Sanskrit Poetics that it represents a systematic tradition which stands separate from that of the orthodox Kasmirian writers and which is followed by Bhoja. It is not a PurÄá¹‡a compiler of such a nature that hints at new paths in special ÅšÄstras and surely the compiler who borrows from TantravÄrttika, Bhartá¹›mitra, Bharata, Daá¹‡á¸in and Ä€nanda, may well borrow from Bhoja, who takes credit for the new Rasa theory propounded by him in his Åšá¹›á¹…gÄraprakÄs’a. The truth therefore is that the Alaá¹…kÄra section in the Agni PurÄá¹‡a is definitely later than Bhoja, from whom it borrowed not only the Ahaá¹…kÄra-AbhimÄna idea of Rasa expounded in his Åšá¹›á¹…gÄraprakÄÅ›a and already referred to in his SarasvatÄ«kaá¹‡á¹­hÄbharaá¹‡a, V. 1, but also some ÅšabdÄlaá¹…kÄras and other ideas.

THE HISTORY OF Vá¹šTTI IN KÄ€VYA

A SURVEY of the concept of Vá¹›tti in the realm of NÄtya where it originated was made by me in an article entitled the Vá¹›ttis in the J.O.R**,** Madras**,** vols**.** VI and VII**.** But like many other concepts**,** the Vá¹›tti passed into KÄvya also**,** experiencing many vicissitudes which form the subject of this chapter**.** If the concept is studied in relation to KÄvya**,** i**.e.,** Åšravya KÄvya**,** in Alaá¹…kÄra ÅšÄstra**,** this is what we must logically expect**:** The whole field of Åšravya KÄvya is BhÄratÄ«Vá¹›tti**.** Descriptions of love**,** evening**,** moonlight**,** seasons etc**.,** must be KaisikÄ«and of war etc**.,** Ä€rabhaá¹­Ä«**.** SÄttvatÄ«**,** if we accept it as the name of action**,** is as absent from KÄvya as BhÄratÄ« is present**.** BhÄratÄ« or the text of the whole KÄvya will be modified**,** according to the situation**,** by Kais’ikÄ«and Ä€rabhaá¹­Ä«**,** producing two main varieties of BhÄratÄ«going by the names VaidarbhÄ« RÄ«ti and Gauá¸Ä«yÄ**,** RÄ«ti**.** The concept of Guá¹‡a must here be related to these**.** The two and the only two Guá¹‡as necessary here for classification are MÄdhurya and Ojas**,** characterising the two extremes of Åšá¹›á¹…gÄra and Raudra**.** The MÄdhurya Guá¹‡a**,** the KaiÅ›ikÄ«Vá¹›tti and the VaidarbhÄ« RÄ«ti will go together on the one hand as distinguishing certain Rasas**,** Äªtivá¹›ttas and verbal expressions**,** and similarly the Ojas Guá¹‡a**,** the Ä€rabhaá¹­Ä«Vá¹›tti and the Gauá¸Ä«RÄ«ti will go together as characteristics of a different set of poetic conditions**.** Guá¹‡a will be the nature of the Rasa**;** Vá¹›tti**,** the nature of Vastu or ideas or Äªtivá¹›tta**;** and RÄ«ti**,** the nature of the expression of

the first and the second in suitable words**.** This**,** in brief**,** must be the simple and strictly logical position of Vá¹›tti in KÄvya**.** But**,** in actual history**,** its career is not found to be so simple**.**

In poetics we have many concepts having the name Vá¹›tti**.** The only one Vá¹›tti with which we have nothing to do here is the à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿**,** the significatory capacities of words**.** The other concepts called Vá¹›tti are three**,** vis**.,** (1) varieties of alliteration**,** à¤…à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤œà¤¾à¤¤à¤¿ (2) varieties of compounded collocation**,** à¤¸à¤®à¤¾à¤¸à¤œà¤¾à¤¤à¤¿**,** and (3) the old Vá¹›ttis**,** Kais’ikÄ« etc**.** of NÄá¹­ya**.**

The first Vá¹›tti is so called because of its harshness**,** the second because of its being refined like the city-bred damsel and the third**,** because it is all soft like an unsophisticated country-bred damsel**.** The third Vá¹›tti**,** GrÄmyÄ**,** is also called KomalÄ**,** signifying the other extreme of the first**,** viz**.,** Puruá¹£Ä**.**

Ä€nandavardhana is very well acquainted with these Vá¹›ttis of Udbhaá¹­a**.** He considers them to be the result of the Guá¹‡as**,** MÄdhurya etc**.** in the collocation**.** (I**,** pp. 5-6.) In Uddyota

three he again mentions the Vá¹›ttis, UpanÄgarikÄ etc. as being such use of words as will promote the realisation of Rasa. He takes the Vá¹›tti in a double sense**,** in the sense of the Vá¹›ttis of NÄtya, KaiÅ›ikÄ« etc. which are to be considered in KÄvya also and in the sense of UpanÄgarikÃ£ etc. The former he describes as ideas suitable or appropriate to Rasa and the latter as words suitable to Rasa (Vide Dhva. Ä€.III**,** p. 182).

Later also Ä€nandavardhana makes the same distinction and mentions the two Vá¹›ttis together**.**

Thus Ä€nandavardhana states more clearly that in KÄvyas there are two Vá¹›ttis**,** the Kais’ikÄ« etc**.** being the same as in NÄá¹­ya and the UpanÄgarikÄ etc**.** which latter**,** from being varieties of AnuprÄsa in Udbhata**,** became à¤°à¤¸à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤—à¥à¤£à¤µà¤°à¥à¤£à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¹à¤¾à¤° and thence in Ä€nandavardhana became more generally à¤°à¤¸à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤—à¥à¤£à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¹à¤¾à¤°**.**

Abhinavagupta also takes Vá¹›ttis as not different essentially from Guá¹‡as**.** He mentions them as they are given by Udbhaá¹­a**,** i.e., as AnuprÄsa varieties**:**

à¤¨à¥ˆà¤µ à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤°à¥€à¤¤à¥€à¤¨à¤¾à¤‚ à¤¤à¤¦à¥(à¤—à¥à¤£)à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿à¤°à¤¿à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤®à¥ à¤¸à¤¿à¤¦à¥à¤§à¤®à¥à¥¤à¤¤à¤¥à¤¾ à¤¹à¤¿ à¤…à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤¾à¤¨à¤¾à¤®à¥‡à¤µ à¤¦à¥€à¤ªà¥à¤¤à¤®à¤¸à¥ƒà¤£à¤®à¤§à¥à¤¯à¤®à¤µà¤°à¥à¤£à¤¨à¥€à¤¯à¥‹à¤ªà¤¯à¥‹à¤—à¤¿à¤¤à¤¯à¤¾ à¤ªà¤°à¥à¤·à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤²à¤²à¤¿à¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤®à¤§à¥à¤¯à¤®à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤µà¤°à¥‚à¤ªà¤µà¤¿à¤µà¥‡à¤šà¤¨à¤¾à¤¯ à¤µà¤°à¥à¤—à¤¤à¥à¤°à¤¯à¤¸à¤‚à¤ªà¤¾à¤¦à¤¨à¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤‚ à¤¤à¤¿à¤¸à¥à¤°à¥‹à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤œà¤¾à¤¤à¤¯à¥‹ à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¯ à¤‡à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¥à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤¾à¤ƒà¥¤à¤µà¤°à¥à¤¤à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¥‡à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤­à¥‡à¤¦à¤¾ à¤†à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¤¿ + + + à¤ªà¤°à¥à¤·à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤ƒ**,** à¤ªà¤°à¥à¤·à¤¾ à¤¦à¥€à¤ªà¥à¤¤à¤¾à¥¤ à¤®à¤¸à¥ƒà¤£à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤ƒà¤‰à¤ªà¤¨à¤¾à¤—à¤°à¤¿à¤•à¤¾**,** à¤¨à¤¾à¤—à¤°à¤¿à¤•à¤¯à¤¾ à¤µà¤¿à¤¦à¤—à¥à¤§à¤¯à¤¾ à¤‰à¤ªà¤®à¤¿à¤¤à¥‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾à¥¤ à¤®à¤§à¥à¤¯à¤®à¤‚ à¤•à¥‹à¤®à¤²à¤®à¤ªà¤°à¥à¤·à¤®à¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤ƒà¥¤à¤…à¤¤ à¤à¤µ à¤µà¥ˆà¤¦à¤—à¥à¤§à¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¿à¤¹à¥€à¤¨à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤•à¥à¤®à¤¾à¤°à¤¾à¤ªà¤°à¥à¤·à¤—à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤®à¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¨à¤¿à¤¤à¤¾à¤¸à¤¾à¤¦à¥ƒà¤¶à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¦à¤¿à¤¯à¤‚ à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤°à¥à¤—à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤®à¥à¤¯à¥‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤š à¤¤à¥ƒà¤¤à¥€à¤¯à¤ƒ à¤•à¥‹à¤®à¤²à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¯à¥‹à¤½à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤œà¤¾à¤¤à¤¯ à¤à¤µà¥¤Locana**,** pp. 5-6**,** N.S. edn**.**

He calls the Paruá¹£Ä**,** DÄ«ptÄ**;** the UpanÄgarikÄ**,** Masá¹›á¹‡Ä or LalitÄ and the GrÄmyÄ**,** MadhyamÄ and KomalÄ**.** Leaving aside the metaphors in the names**,** one can see that the Paruá¹£Äsuits VÄ«ra**,** Raudra and BÄ«bhatsa Rasas and can go with the Ä€rabhaá¹­Ä«Vá¹›tti**;** the UpanÄgarikÄ and KomalÄ suit S’á¹›á¹…gÄra and HÄsya and can go with the Kais’ikÄ« Vá¹›tti**.** Abhinavagupta says in a later context**:**

à¤¨à¤¾à¤—à¤°à¤¿à¤•à¤¯à¤¾ à¤¹à¥à¤¯à¤ªà¤°à¤®à¤¤à¥‡ (à¤¹à¥à¤¯à¥à¤ªà¤®à¤¿à¤¤à¤¾) à¤…à¤¨à¥à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¸à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤ƒ à¤¶à¥ƒà¤™à¥à¤—à¤¾à¤°à¤¾à¤¦à¥Œ à¤µà¤¿à¤¶à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤®à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿à¥¤à¤ªà¤°à¥à¤·à¥‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¦à¥€à¤ªà¥à¤¤à¥‡à¤·à¥ à¤°à¥Œà¤¦à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¦à¤¿à¤·à¥à¥¤ à¤•à¥‹à¤®à¤²à¥‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¹à¤¾à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¦à¥Œà¥¤ à¤¤à¤¥à¤¾ â€”**â€˜à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¯à¤ƒ à¤•à¤¾à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤®à¤¾à¤¤à¥ƒà¤•à¤¾à¤ƒâ€™**à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¯à¤¦à¥à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤‚ à¤®à¥à¤¨à¤¿à¤¨à¤¾ à¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤° à¤°à¤¸à¥‹à¤šà¤¿à¤¤ à¤à¤µ à¤šà¥‡à¤·à¥à¤Ÿà¤¾à¤µà¤¿à¤¶à¥‡à¤·à¥‹ à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤ƒà¥¤

p. 232**,** III. Locana**,** N. S. Edn**.**

Thus Abhinavagupta considers both the Vá¹›ttis as Rasaucita-vyavahÄra**,** the one**,** KaÅ›ikÄ« etc**.,** of Artha or ideas and the other**,** UpanÄgarikÄ etc**.,** of Åšabda**,** words or letters**.**Therefore in KÄvya we will not have a classification of à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤

and à¤…à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿among Kais’ikyÄdivá¹›ttis themselves**.** BhÄratÄ«will not be a à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿**.** It also becomes an Artha VyavahÄra or Artha Vá¹›tti**.** All the four are Artha Vá¹›ttis and as distinguished from them**,** the S’abda Vá¹›ttis are the three**,** UpanÄgarikÄ etc.

If Åšabda and Artha are thus distributed between UpanÄgarikÄ etc**.** on the one hand and Kais’ikÄ«etc. on the other**,** what shall RÄ«ti stand for ? Ä€nandavardhana does separately mention RÄ«ti along with the Vá¹›ttis UpanÄgarikÄ etc**.** in both the contexts noted above**,** in Uddyotas one and three**.** In Uddyota one**,** he**,** as interpreted by Abhinavagupta (Vide pp.5-6), holds RÄ«tis also as dependent on Guá¹‡as like the Vá¹›ttis**,** UpanÄgarikÄ etc**.** But strictly speaking there is no room for RÄ«ti in either Ä€nandavardhana’s scheme or Abhinavagupta’s**.** For**,** RÄ«ti can be à¤°à¤¸à¥‹à¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¹à¤¾à¤°â€” such use of words as are appropriate to Rasa but that place has been given to the Vá¹›ttis**,** UpanÄgarÄ±kÄ etc**.** which have come to mean not exactly varieties of AnuprÄsa but use of words suitable to Rasa**.** Therefore it is no wonder that we soon see in Mammaá¹­a the equation of the three RÄ«tis**,** VaidarbhÄ«**,** Gauá¸Ä«and PÄÃ±cÄlÄ« with the three Vá¹›ttis UpanÄgarikÄ**,** Paruá¹£Ä and KomalÄ. Mammaá¹­a says that AnuprÄsa is firstly of two kinds**,** Cheka and Vá¹›tti AnuprÄsa and that the latter is the arrangement of letters suitable to Rasa.

We see here that**,** though Bhoja does not use here the names UpanÄgarikÄ**,** NÄgarikÄ and GrÄmyÄ**,** he uses still the names LalitÄ**,** Paruá¹£Äand KomalÄand to these three adds nine more**.** After illustrating these he refutes them all**.** He opines that such Vá¹›ttis are unnecessary since they are not separate from either the Guá¹‡as or the Vá¹›ttis**,** Kais’ikÄ«etc.

Having cast away this Vá¹›tti (i.e., the old AnuprÄsa JÄtis increased into twelve), Bhoja holds another set of twelve AnuprÄsaJÄtisas being called Vá¹›tti or Vá¹›ttyanuprÄsa. THE HISTORY OF AUCITYA IN SANSKRIT POETICS

One of the noteworthy points in the Sanskrit systems of literary criticism is that, in an inquiry into a comprehensive philosophy of the literary art, they do not separate poetry and drama, nor prose and verse. Bharata, in his NÄá¹­ya ÅšÄstra, has defined Drama as Imitation of the three worlds or representation of the actions of men of various nature: à¤¤à¥à¤°à¥ˆà¤²à¥‹à¤•à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¿à¤ƒ or à¤§à¥€à¤°à¥‹à¤¦à¤¾à¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤¥à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¤¿à¤ƒ (N, Åš, 107, 113, 120 etc. Vide also DaÅ›arÅ«paka I, 7). Consequently Bharata has perfected a system of ideas of â€˜Loka DharmÄ«â€™, which term means â€˜the ways of the worldâ€™or to put it short â€˜Natureâ€™, and stands to denote the realistic elements in Bharataâ€™s Stage.61 and NÄá¹­ya Dharmi (Conventions and Idealism) of Bharataâ€™s Stage in the JOR, Madras, Vol. VII.")In the concept of Praká¹›ti, Bharata studies the various kinds of men, minds, and natures found in the worlds. In the concept of Pravá¹›iti he has studied the provincial, racial, and national characteristics in dressing and other activities. He has elaborately dealt with Ä€hÄrya-abhinaya, dress and make-up, which, he says, must be appropriate to the Rasa and BhÄva.

à¤¯à¤¥à¤¾à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤°à¤¸à¤¾à¤µà¤¸à¥à¤¥à¤‚ à¤µà¤¿à¤œà¥à¤à¤¾à¤¯à¥ˆà¤µà¤‚ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¯à¥‹à¤œà¤¯à¥‡à¤¤à¥à¥¥ N. Åš. XXIII, 42.

He has devoted separate sections to a consideration of the most proper way of correct speaking in the drama according to the emotions (XIX, à¤ªà¤¾à¤ à¥à¤¯à¤—à¥à¤£à¤¾à¤ƒ), of the Svaras suitable for each mood and of the musical tunes, JÄtyamÅ›akas, appropriate to the varying Rasa and BhÄva (XXIX, 1-4). These remarks apply to the artists of the stage and theatre, the actors, theconductor and others. Regarding the work of the poetdramatist, Bharata has analysed the text of the drama and has pointed out how the verbal qualities of sweetness, harshness etc., and the flights of fancies expressed in the form of figures of speech have to be appropriate to that BhÄva or Rasa which is portrayed (XVII, 108-123). Thus at the end of the treatment of each topic, Bharata has an important section called â€˜Rasa-prayogaâ€™, where he points out what suits what.

So much so that Bharata observes that, in judging drama, the ground of reference for success of the art is the world. He emphasises that one has to know the infinite variety of human natureâ€”Praká¹›ti and ÅšÄ«la, on which is NÄá¹­ya or drama based.

à¤¨à¥‹à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤¾à¤¨à¤¿ à¤š à¤®à¤¯à¤¾ à¤¯à¤¾à¤¨à¤¿ à¤²à¥‹à¤•à¤—à¥à¤°à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¨à¤¿ à¤¤à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¤ªà¤¿à¥¤

(end of the chapter on dress and make-up). Nature or the three worlds or Praká¹›ti or ÅšÄ«laâ€” all these can finally be referred to by the single word Rasa which is the â€˜Soulâ€™of poetry. Drama is the representation of moods, BhÄva-anukÄ«rtana, as Bharata puts it. Out of these moods flow everythingâ€”the actions, the character, the dress, the nature of one’s speech etc. Thus to this factor, which is at the root of all these things, viz., Rasa, have these things again to be referredfor finding out whether in representing them, there is propriety or appropriateness. Things cannot be estimated by themselves separately and labelled as good or bad, appealing or otherwise. That is, Guá¹‡atva and Doá¹£atva do not inherently pertain to anything eternally but anything, according to the situation where it occurs, is either suitable or not; and in this suitability or otherwise lies Guá¹‡atva or Doá¹£atva. What Bharata says of ornaments and decoration in the make-up of the characters is true of all other parts of the art of representation by the poet and the production of the drama on the stage by the actors. Bharata lays down that if a thing does not agree or is not proper in a certain place with reference to Rasa, it is the greatest literary flaw. Improper placing, like placing a necklace at the foot and an anklet round the neck, can only produce laughter.

à¤…à¤¦à¥‡à¤¶à¤œà¥‹ à¤¹à¤¿ à¤µà¥‡à¤·à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¥ à¤¨ à¤¶à¥‹à¤­à¤¾à¤‚ à¤œà¤¨à¤¯à¤¿à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¿à¥¤
à¤®à¥‡à¤–à¤²à¥‹à¤°à¤¸à¤¿ à¤¬à¤¨à¥à¤§à¥‡à¤š à¤¹à¤¾à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¥ˆà¤µà¥‹à¤ªà¤œà¤¾à¤¯à¤¤à¥‡à¥¥ N. Åš., XXIII, 69.

It is a serious breach of propriety for a writer to describe a forlorn lady suffering from separation from her lord (i.e., one in PravÄsa Vipralambha) as having her body fully decked with jewels. In the realm of artistic expression the same rule holds good. A poet commits the greatest crime against Rasa if he introduces a cartload of ornaments of a verbal character in places where Rasa has to be effectively portrayed and where the absence of any figure is itself the perfection of art. The proper placing of things in such a manner as to suit Rasa and the avoiding of things not suitable form the essence of artistic expression. This is propriety, Aucitya. An anklet adds no beauty as an ornament but an anklet as an ornament for the ankle is helpful to beautify one. We can thus see how this doctrine of appropriateness, propriety and adaptationâ€” all comprehended in the one word Aucitya, is directly derivable from Bharata. Just put by the side of the verse of Bharata abovequoted, the verse illustrative of the theory of Aucitya given by Ká¹£emendra in his AucityavicÄracarcÄ, in which work the doctrine of Aucitya had the complete elaboration into a system of criticism, and see :

Thus the first work in the history of Sanskrit Poetics contains implicitly as much of this theory of Aucitya of the Sanskrit Alaá¹…kara ÅšÄstra, as of the other theory of poetry, Rasa, explicitly, even though emphasis on both theseâ€” Aucitya and Rasaâ€”was again systematically laid only as late as the ninth, tenth and eleventh centuries.

Aucitya is harmony and in one aspect it is proportion between the whole and the parts, between chief and the subsidiary, between the Aá¹…gin and the Angas. This perfection is all the morals and beauty in art. At the final stage of its formulation as a theory explaining the secret of poetic appeal, Aucitya is stated to be the â€˜JÄ«vitaâ€™, life-breath, of poetry. This Aucitya, which is proportion and harmony on one side and appropriateness and adaptation on the other, cannot be understood by itself but presupposes that to which all other things are harmonious and appropriate. Surely there has to be harmony and appropriateness in every part and between one part and another; but everything as a whole has to be pronounced proper and appropriate or otherwise by a reference to what constitutes the â€˜Soulâ€™â€” Ä€tman of poetry viz., Rasa. Thus Bharata speaks of the Rasa-prayoga of Pravá¹›tti, Vá¹›tti, Guá¹‡a, AlaikÄra, Ä€hÄryÄbhinaya, PÄá¹­hyaguá¹‡a, Svara and JÄtyamÅ›a. In later terminology, this Rasaprayoga is Rasa-aucitya. But Aucitya is only implicitly contained in Bharata. It was only rather late that Poetics got itself again wedded and identified with Bharataâ€™s Dramaturgy and took its stand scientifically on the two pedestals of Rasa and Aucitya, which it had forgotten for a time, as we shall now see in the following account of the history of the concept of Aucitya after Bharata.

MÄgha

The next glimpse we have of Aucitya is in MÄgha, who, in his poem, has made some side-remarks which shoot their rays into the darkness of the early

history of Poetics. In canto ii of MÄghaâ€™s ÅšiÅ›upÄlavadha, we have a verse on the policy best suited for the king, which, through comparison, drags in the topic of Guá¹‡as in KÄvyas or dramas.

à¤¤à¥‡à¤œà¤ƒ à¤•à¥à¤·à¤®à¤¾ à¤µà¤¾ à¤¨à¥ˆà¤•à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¤‚ à¤•à¤¾à¤²à¤œà¥à¤à¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤®à¤¹à¥€à¤ªà¤¤à¥‡à¤ƒà¥¤
à¤¨à¥ˆà¤•à¤®à¥‹à¤œà¤ƒà¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¸à¤¾à¤¦à¥‹ à¤¬à¤¾ à¤°à¤¸à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤µà¤¿à¤¦à¤ƒ à¤•à¤µà¥‡à¤ƒà¥¥ Åš. V. II, 83.

The king has to achieve his purpose with an eye on expediency. Time and circumstance are the pre-eminently deciding factors of his policy. There is no inherent good in either power or forbearance and peace by themselves but all goodness of a policy consists in its effectiveness, in using that which is suited to the time. Prowess is waste and will even ruin the cause where it is needlessly flaunted. Forbearance cannot help the king when he has succeed by putting up athick fight. Thus, adaptation is the only policy good for the king. The case is similar to that of a poet with whom the main concern is Rasa and BhÄva and an understanding of their subtle nature. In portraying his characters and their actions and in describing them, it will not do if the poet sticks to one quality throughout, say PrasÄda or Ojas. When the VÄ«ra, Adbhuta and Raudra Rasas appear, he has to adopt the Guá¹‡a Ojas to suit the vigour, energy and blaze (DÄ«pti) of those Rasas and when the key of emotion is lowered and quiet emotinal effects have to be produced, the requisite quality for the poet is PrasÄda. Thus, not Guá¹‡as by themselves, but that Guá¹‡a which is proper and appropriateâ€”Ucitaâ€”is helpful to Rasa. This is Guá¹‡a-aucitya. Aucitya is here Adaptation. MÄgha, as a poet, had this clear insight into Bharataâ€™s ideas of Rasa and Guá¹‡as appropriate to each Rasa. Bhoja considers such appropriateness in expression between the emotion and the stylistic quality as a Prabandha-guá¹‡a,

i.e., one of the good features of good poetry. He calls it â€˜à¤°à¤¸à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤°à¥‚à¤ªà¤¸à¤¨à¥à¤¦à¤°à¥à¤­à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤®à¥â€™. He means the same thing as what MÄgha says in the above-given verse, which also Bhoja quotes.

In the first half of the 8th century, King YaÅ›ovarman of Kanauj, patron of BhavabhÅ«ti, wrote his drama RÄmÄbhyudaya, whose prologue has some interest to the student of the history of Poetics for a verse in it on certain concepts connected with theoretical literary criticism. Lollaá¹­a

In practice, as can be seen from the numerous and large MahÄkÄvyas, which are entitled to that name because of their bulk at least, all notions of propriety had become unknown to poets. The several limbs over-developed themselves separately, like elephantiac leg, and the KÄvya as a whole was an outrage on harmony and Aucitya. This Lollaá¹­a severely criticised, perhaps in his commentary on the NÄá¹­ya ÅšÄstra. To this aspect of Aucitya viz., proportion and strict relevancy of every detail, Lollata drew attention. proportion. Ornaments hide beauty if they are not structural or organic; similarly â€˜descriptionsâ€™ have to logically emerge out of the story and the complex course of its Rasa as a necessity. Descriptive cantos should not stand out like outhouses and isolated places for the poet’s mind to indulge at length in excess. This is true of the drama as much as of the epic poem. In a drama, the sub-plots, the PatÄkÄ and the PrakarÄ« and the Sandhyaá¹…gas should not be considered by themselves as having any virtue but should be seen to be relevant to Rasa. Rudraá¹­a

From Daá¹‡á¸in we had to come to Lollaá¹­a before we could again catch sight of Aucitya as a principle underlying many literary dicta. This means that we have to come almost to the time of Ä€nandavardhana whom Rudraá¹­a must have slightly preceded. Up to the time of Rudraá¹­a the concept was developing unconsciously without a name. The name Aucitya was not given to the idea by any writer of poetic theory, and one more useful word was not thus added to the critical vocabulary of the Sahá¹›daya. But the word Aucitya must have slowly dawned in the circles of Sahá¹›dayas and we first see that word used in theoretical literature only in Rudraá¹­a’s KÄvyÄlaá¹…kÄra, a work which has not yet left the primitive Alaá¹…kÄra-stage

of criticism but has however embodied into itself a good deal of the concept of Rasa, which alone, according to it, made poetry that interesting and charming thing it isâ€” Sarasa. The word Aucitya occurs often in Ä€nandavardhana’s work and Rudraá¹­a is only the first writer to mention it in theoretical literature. For, earlier, in the first half of the eighth century, King YaÅ›ovarman of Kanauj uses the word Aucitya with much theoretical significance, in much the same significance as the word is used with in later times, in the prologue of his lost drama, RÄmÄbhyudaya, as we have noticed above. Thus the three stages to be noticed in the appearance of the name Aucitya is its mention by YaÅ›ovarman, treatment of it to a small extent in Rudraá¹­a and to a large extent in Ä€nanda-vardhana’s DhvanyÄloka. Rudraá¹­a just preceded Ä€nandavardhana or was an early contemporary of his. He was perhaps writing in Åšaá¹…kuka’s time. Some ideas given in the Dhva. Ä€. are already seen in Rudrata’s work. Many of the Rasa doá¹£as mentioned by Ä€nandavardhana under Rasaucitya in Uddyota iii are found in Rudraá¹­a’s K. A. What we must note here at present is that though Rudraá¹­a treats of Alaá¹…kÄras so largely and though his work is yet one of the old period in which works are called KÄvya-Alaá¹…kÄra, he has realised the importance of Rasa to suit which Alaá¹…kÄras exist. If Alaá¹…kÄras are otherwise, they have little meaning. That is what Ä€nandavar-dhana develops in a section on Alaá¹…kÄrasamÄ«ká¹£Ä in Uddyota ii. The idea that Rasa and Rasaucitya control Alaá¹…kÄras is already seen in Rudraá¹­a, who, as said above, is the first writer of Poetics to mention the word Aucitya. After dealing with some ÅšabdÄlankÄras like Yamakas which are a siren to the easily tempted poets, Rudraá¹­a says, by way of closing the chapter, that these figures must be introduced after bestowing due thought on propriety, Aucitya, with reference to the main

theme. Even the AnuprÄsas have to be now cast away and now taken and must be sparsely used with much advantage. They must not be thickly overlaid upon the theme through the whole length of it.

That Dhvani is the only artistic process by which Rasa, the â€˜Ä€tmanâ€™, is portrayed by the poet and is got at by the Sahá¹›daya and that everywhere things appeal most by being deftly concealed and suggested by suppression in a fabric of symbology, are the reasons why Ä€nandavardhana posits Dhvani as the â€˜Ä€tmanâ€™of poetry. Firstly, Alaá¹…kÄra by itself has no virtue. It has to be relevant, helpful to develop Rasa and never an overgrowth hindering or making hideous the poem. The term Alaá¹…kÄra itself has meaning only then.

The topic of Aucitya of Alaá¹…kÄra giving the rules which alone secure the appropriate employing of Alaá¹…kÄra is dealt with by Ä€nandavardhana in Ud. II, ÅšIs. 15-20, pp. 85-93. The rationale of Ä€nandavardhana’s principles is this whatever the poet writes must be suggestive of Rasa and everything has to be tested good or bad, relevant or irrelevant,

beautiful or ugly, by applying this strict logic of their capacity to suggest or hinder Rasa. The main refrain of Ä€nandavardhana here is that Alankara should be structural, organically emerging as the only way of expressing an emotion and it must never be a cold and deliberate effort at decoration, necessitating the forgetting of Rasa and the taking of a special effort.

Similarly Ä€nandavardhana relates Guá¹‡a to Rasa of which Guá¹‡a is the â€˜Dharmaâ€™ and points out Aucitya of Guá¹‡a. The quality of MÄdhurya is inherent in Åšá¹›ngÄra, Vipralambha and Karuá¹‡a, whereas Raudra is attended by the quality of DÄ«pti, by a blazing up of the hearts. Accordingly words and collocation used in the two different cases must be such as to agree with the mood and the atmosphere of the Guá¹‡a and its Rasa or such as to suggest the Guá¹‡a and the Rasa. Thus sweet sound effects, the soft letters with nasal conjunct consonants, suggest and promote the realisation of the more tender and sweeter emotional moods whereas harsh combinations which jar in the above instances instil vigour and become very appropriate to or highly suggestive of the wild Rasa of Raudra. This proper use of letters is Vará¹‡a-aucitya; Ä€nandavardhana will say that there is Varnadhvani in these instances; and a third will call it Vará¹‡avakratÄ. Collocation suggestive of

The section on PrabandhadhvanÄ± deals with the very substance of a poem or drama and here one has to see that everything observes the principles of Aucitya and justifies itself suggesting, as best as it can, the Rasa. A story has to be built as the expression of a Rasa. If a story already available is handled, changes suitable to the Rasa must be made wherever the old story is not helpful to bring out the Rasa. If there are too many incidents, only those that are most expressive of the emotion must be chosen. This is Prabandhadhvani and Prabandhaucitya as also Prakaraá¹‡adhvani

and Prakaraá¹‡aucitya to adopt the two-fold classification of Kuntaka. Bhoja would call this appropriate change in the story as Prabandhadoá¹£ahÄna and Kuntaka as Prakaraá¹‡avakratÄ. Appropriateness of which suggestiveness is the touch-stone is meant by all these writers. Says Ä€nandavardhana:

In Harivijaya, when the delicate sentiment of Vipralambha has to be delineated, the poet has succumbed to the temptation of an overdone description of the beach and the sea. Bharata himself recognises how each part and incident in the drama has to refer to Rasa and how, otherwise, it has no right to exist. It is only natural, for Bharata is the writer who lays the greatest emphasis on Rasa to which everything else is subservient. Ä€nandavardhana observes that, simply because Bharata has laid down a certain number of emotional points or incidents as Sandhyangas, one must not try to see that he introduces everything mentioned by Bharata. Whatever is introduced must be on the score of its suggestiveness of Rasa and not on the score of loyalty to text.

Ä€nandavardhana then goes to other kinds of Aucitya or ather points out how, not only the working out of a plot, not only the expression of an idea in figure, but even the words and the synonyms, the case, inflection, voice etc., have to be suggestive of Rasa. All these ideas revolve round Aucitya. If Sup, Tiá¹…g, KÄraka etc., are suggestive, they are â€˜ucita’, appropriate.

Abhinavagupta

The place of Abhinavagupta in the history of Aucitya is important. As the author of the Locana he lucidly expounds and elaborates the ideas of Ä€nandavardhana, who, as we have seen above, is the greatest name in the history of Aucitya. On the other side, Abhinavagupta is the teacher in Poetics65 of Ká¹£emendra who is the systematiser of Aucitya. It is clear from Ä€nandavardhana’s treatment of Aucitya in Ud. III, that Aucitya naturally emerges out of the doctrines of Rasa and Dhvani and that the three cannot be separated. Abhinavagupta takes his stand on this triple aspect of the â€˜lifeâ€™ of poetryâ€” Rasa first, then Dhvani and then Aucitya. He says:

à¤‰à¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¥‡à¤¨ à¤°à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤·à¤¯à¤®à¥Œà¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤­à¤µà¤¤à¥€à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¦à¤°à¥à¤¶à¤¯à¤¨à¥ à¤°à¤¸à¤§à¥à¤µà¤¨à¥‡à¤ƒ à¤œà¥€à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤‚ à¤¸à¥‚à¤šà¤¯à¤¤à¤¿à¥¤ p. 13.

Aucitya presupposes something to which a thing is â€˜ucitaâ€™and that to which everything else is finally to be estimated as â€˜ucitaâ€™is Rasa which is the â€˜soulâ€™of poetry.

On the subject of Alaá¹…kÄraucitya about which Ä€nanda-vardhana speaks so much in Ud. II, Abhinavagupta says that the greatest Aucitya of Alaá¹…kÄra is that the term has any meaning at all only when there is the ‘Alaá¹…kÄrya’, the ‘soul’. Otherwise, it is like decorating the dead body. Decoration of a living body also is Anaucitya in certain cases, ornaments on the body of a recluse who has renounced life appear ridiculousâ€” anucita. Thus figures of speech without Rasa and figures of speech in places which do not need them are bad.

à¤µà¤¿à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¥Œà¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¥‡à¤¨ à¤¹à¤¿ à¤µà¤¿à¤¨à¤¾ à¤•à¤¾ à¤°à¤¸à¤µà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¾ à¤•à¤µà¥‡à¤°à¤¿à¤¤à¤¿à¥¤ à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤®à¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤µà¤¿à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤¾à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¥Œà¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤®à¥‡à¤µ à¤°à¤¸à¤µà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¾à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¯à¥‹à¤œà¤•à¤‚ à¤¨à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¤¦à¤¿à¤¤à¤¿ à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤ƒà¥¤

The idea of Aucitya, like that of Vakrokti, was current as a very frequently used term in the critical circles of Kashmirian Ä€laá¹…kÄrikas for a long time. Vakrokti rose out of Alaá¹…kÄra, Aucitya in the wake of Rasa and Dhvani. Aucitya must have become more current after Ä€nandavardhana who has spoken of it so much and who has said that its presence and absence makes and unmakes Rasa and poetry. It was so much in use that, by the time of Abhinavagupta, it must have been heading towards systematisation, even as the concept of Vakrokti, which, as old as BhÄmaha, was given so much life in the critical circles that it enlarged itself and through Kuntaka built itself into a system. Aucitya also had assumed proportions and was in search of a writer for systematisation. The

critics were speaking of Aucitya as the essence of poetry very often, more often than Rasa even. Says Abhinavagupta in two places criticising these critics: ‘One cannot be indiscreetly using the word Aucitya by itself; Aucitya is ununder-standable without something else to which things are â€œucitaâ€â€” appropriate. Aucitya is a relation and that to which things are or should be in that relation must first be grasped. That is Rasa, nothing less and nothing else.’ Abhinavagupta first proves that there is no meaning in Aucitya without Rasa.

à¤‰à¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¥‡à¤¨ à¤°à¤¸à¤µà¤¿à¤·à¤¯à¤®à¥Œà¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤­à¤µà¤¤à¥€à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¦à¤°à¥à¤¶à¤¯à¤¨à¥ à¤°à¤¸à¤§à¥à¤µà¤¨à¥‡à¤ƒ à¤œà¥€à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤‚ à¤¸à¥‚à¤šà¤¯à¤¤à¤¿à¥¤ à¤¤à¤¦à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¥‡ à¤¹à¤¿ à¤•à¤¿à¤®à¤ªà¥‡à¤•à¥à¤·à¤¯à¥‡à¤¦à¤®à¥Œà¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤¨à¤¾à¤® à¤¸à¤°à¥à¤µà¤¤à¥à¤° à¤‰à¤¦à¥à¤§à¥‹à¤·à¥à¤¯à¤¤ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤ƒà¥¤ p. 13.

He again proves that Aucitya presupposes Rasa, and Dhvani also.

à¤”à¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¤à¥€ (à¤…à¤¤à¤¿à¤¶à¤¯à¥‹à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤ƒ) à¤œà¥€à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¤®à¤¿à¤¤à¤¿ à¤šà¥‡à¤¤à¥, à¤”à¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤¨à¤¿à¤¬à¤¨à¥à¤§à¤¨à¤‚ à¤°à¤¸à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤¾à¤¦à¤¿ à¤®à¥à¤•à¥à¤¤à¥à¤µà¤¾à¤¨à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¥ à¤•à¤¿à¤à¥à¤šà¤¿à¤¦à¤¸à¥à¤¤à¥€à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¤à¤¦à¥‡à¤µà¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¤°à¥à¤­à¤¾à¤¸à¤¿ à¤®à¥à¤–à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤œà¥€à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¤®à¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤­à¥à¤¯à¥à¤ªà¤—à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤‚, à¤¨ à¤¤à¥ à¤¸à¤¾à¥¤ à¤à¤¤à¥‡à¤¨ à¤¯à¤¦à¤¾à¤¹à¥à¤ƒ à¤•à¥‡à¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥, â€˜à¤”à¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤˜à¤Ÿà¤¿à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤¨à¥à¤¦à¤°à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤®à¤¯à¥‡ à¤•à¤¾à¤µà¥à¤¯à¥‡ à¤•à¤¿à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¥‡à¤¨ à¤§à¥à¤µà¤¨à¤¿à¤¨à¤¾ à¤†à¤¤à¥à¤®à¤­à¥‚à¤¤à¥‡à¤¨ à¤•à¤²à¥à¤ªà¤¿à¤¤à¥‡à¤¨â€™ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¸à¥à¤µà¤µà¤šà¤¨à¤®à¥‡à¤µ à¤§à¥à¤µà¤¨à¤¿à¤¸à¤¦à¥à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤¾à¤­à¥à¤¯à¥à¤ªà¤—à¤®à¤¸à¤¾à¤•à¥à¤·à¤¿à¤­à¥‚à¤¤à¤®à¥ à¤…à¤®à¤¨à¥à¤¯à¤®à¤¾à¤¨à¤¾à¤ƒ à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¥à¤•à¥à¤¤à¤¾à¤ƒà¥¤

p. 208. Locana.

These two passages clearly show that critics there were who were speaking of Aucitya as the only thing enough to explain poetry, which according to them, was beautiful words and ideas set in perfect harmony â€” Aucitya. These critics had omitted the word Rasa from their vocabulary and dispensed with Dhvani. Abhinavagupta criticises these poor critics who do not understand the implication of what they say. Aucitya implies, presupposes and means ‘suggestion of Rasa ‘â€”à¤°à¤¸à¤§à¥à¤µà¤¨à¤¿ i.e., the doctrines of Rasa and Dhvani.

Abhinavagupta thus takes his stand on the tripod of Rasa, Dhvani and Aucitya. Rasa is the ‘Ä€tman’ of poetry and the fact is that it is so only through the process of Dhvani. Again Rasa is or can be so only through Aucitya. Thus these three are very intimately and inseparably associated together. Aucitya is as inseparably associated with Dhvani as with Rasa. If an Alaá¹…kÄra is said to suit, to be ‘ucita’ to, a BhÄva, it means that the Alaá¹…kÄra effectively suggests that BhÄva; if there is said to be Guá¹‡aucitya, it means the Rasa there is suggested by the Guá¹‡a. A word, a gender, a mere exclamationâ€” these are said to be â€˜ucitaâ€™, and how? The test. of Aucitya, its proof, is the suggestion of Rasa.

Another point which Abhinavagupta pointed out was that the breach of Aucitya resulted in ‘Ä€bhÄsatÄ.’ A KÄvya which does not have Aucitya is KÄvyÄbhÄsa, not poetry but semblance of poetry. Improper Alaá¹…kÄra is AlankÄrÄbhÄsa. If there is Aucitya we have Rasa and sentiment; if there is Anaucitya due to absence of Praká¹›tyaucitya etc., we have RasÄbhÄsa and sentimentality.

Kuntaka

Kuntaka naturally speaks much of Aucitya which, we are given to understand by the Locana, was a term widely current in circles of Sahá¹›dayas of that time. Kuntaka was a younger contemporary of Abhinavagupta or wroteimmediately after him. The word denoting the essence of poetry at that time seems to be ‘Jivita’. For we find the Locana itself rendering the â€˜Ä€tmanâ€™of Ä€nandavardhana as â€˜Jivita’ twice. Kuntaka uses the same word ‘Jivita’ to praise his Vakrokti and soon Ksemendra is to use the same to signify the place of Aucitya. The two main facts recognised by Kuntaka in poetry are the utterance and its embellishment or its strikingness called Alaá¹…kÄra or Vakrokti. Besides these, he recognises certain general concepts which go to define his notion of poetry. Notable among these is the idea of SÄhitya. Along with SÄhitya, Kuntaka mentions two â€˜SÄdhÄraá¹‡a Guá¹‡as’ called Aucitya and SaubhÄgya. These general excellences pertaining to all styles of poetry are to be distinguished from the â€˜AsÄdhÄraá¹‡a Guá¹‡as’, special qualities, which go to distinguish styles into the graceful (sukumÄra), the striking (victra), and the middling (madhyama). The SÄdhÄraá¹‡a Guá¹‡as, Aucitya and SaubhÄgya, are of greater importance.

Kuntaka grasps the supreme importance of Rasa and character, i.e., Praká¹›ti or, as Kuntaka often says, SvabhÄva. Mahimabhaá¹­á¹­a

Mahimabhaá¹­á¹­a wrote in the same age, just after Abhinavagupta and Kuntaka. MahimÄ accepts Rasa supreme and also the Aucitya pertaining to Rasa, BhÄva and Praká¹›ti. He could not escape the idea of Aucitya which was in its season then. As his criticism of Kuntaka’s definition of poetry by VakroktÄ± shows, critics of his time were aware of only two things as specially distinguishing the poetic utterance from the ordinary or ÅšÄstraic one, viz., Aucitya and Dhvani. Of these two, there is no need to specially

speak of the former because MahimÄconsiders it as the supreme necessity in so far as KÄvya is accepted as utterance ensouled by Rasa. That is, according to MahimÄ, there can be no opposition to Aucitya. It is only with Dhvani that he fights.

On the point of Rasa and the Aucitya of every element of expression to this Rasa, MahimÄ is completely in agreement with Ä€nandavardhana. Ä€nandavardhana says that if there is one word which is NÄ«rasa, devoid of Rasa, it is the greatest literary flaw, the ApaÅ›abda. Similarly all flaws are comprised in one common flaw, viz., hindrance to the realisation of Rasa. All Doá¹£as are hindrances to Rasa and MahimÄcalls them by the common name Anaucitya. He quotes Ä€nandavardhana’s memorable KÄrikÄ on this subject.

Certain ideas get certain writers as their brilliant exponents. Thus SÄhitya gets Kuntaka as its first great exponent. To MahimÄfalls the share of expounding two ideas, SvabhÄvokti and Doá¹£as. The most important part of MahimÄ’s work is chapter II of his V. V., devoted to a study of five important flaws of expression, on which the classic KÄvya PrakÄÅ›a, the model for later compilations, draws for its own Doá¹£aprakarana to a great extent. These five flaws, and all others also are only the many varieties of Anaucitya which means hindrance to RasapratÄ«ti. For Aucitya of Rasa and Praká¹›ti is the greatest Guá¹‡a, most essential for KÄvya. The absence of this Aucitya is the greatest Doá¹£a within which every other Dosa is included. Aucitya and Anaucitya pertain to the content, i.e., Rasa and Artha or Vastu, as well as to the outer garment of the Rasa and Vastu, viz., the expressionâ€” Åšabda. The former is Ä€bhyantara or Antaraá¹…gaâ€” internal, while the latter is Bahirangaâ€” external. Even the unsuggestive or inappropriate metre is an Anaucitya, one belonging to the latter category. Among ÅšabdÄnaucityas, MahimÄ says that five are to be specially noted; they are five Doá¹£as named VidheyÄvimarÅ›a, Prakrama-bheda, Kramabheda, Paunaruktya and VÄcyÄvacana.

Ká¹£emendra

Ká¹£emendra was the pupil of Ä€cÄrva Abhinavagupta in poetics. Ká¹£emendra first wrote a work on Poetics called Kavikará¹‡ikÄ69which is unfortunately lost to us. Perhaps in it he dealt with Rasa and Dhvani. Our sense of its loss is keen because, in his critical writings spared to us we find many a touch of originality. Ká¹£emendra’s Kavikaá¹‡á¹­hÄbharaá¹‡a and Suvá¹›ttatilaka have only slight and subsidiary interest for us. It is his Aucitya-vicÄracarcÄwe are concerned here with, a small work which yet belongs to the class of â€˜PrasthÄna-worksâ€™ like those of BhÄmaha, Daá¹‡á¸in, VÄmana, Ä€nandavardhana. Kuntaka and Mahimabhaá¹­á¹­a. As is plain from the above-gone survey of the concept of Aucitya, Ká¹£emendra is not the author of Aucitya, but, as in the case of Vakrokti and Kuntaka, Ká¹£emendra made Aucitya into a system, elaborating that concept and applying it to all parts of the KÄvya. Ká¹£emendra only worked out Ä€nandavardhana and Abhinavagupta in whose system he had his being. Abhinavagupta criticised those critics who glibly talked of Aucitya without reference to Rasa and Dhvani which alone render Aucitya intelligible. Just as Kuntaka’s Vakrokti proceeds only after accepting Rasa as supreme and accepts also Dhvani, so also Ká¹£emendra’s Aucitya. Ká¹£emendra first posits Rasa as the soul of poetry, as the thing whose presence makes Kavya; Aucitya is its lifeâ€” â€˜JÄ«vita’. The term ‘JÄ«vita’,as can be seen from the two quotations given above, was used

by Abhinavagupta to denote Rasadhvani with Aucitya. Thus Abhinavagupta used both the words ‘Ä€tman’ and ‘JÄ«vita’ as interchangeable and as meaning generally the essenceâ€” à¤¸à¤¾à¤°à¤­à¥‚à¤¤à¥‹à¤½à¤°à¥à¤¥à¤ƒ. But Ká¹£emendra made a subtle distinction between Soul and Life, Rasa theÄ€tman and Aucitya the Life.^(1)These two metaphorical names and the relation between them in metaphysical speculations point to the fact of the intimate relation between Rasa and Aucitya and of how both come into existence together. Ká¹£emendra’s attitude to Rasa is thus plainly stated even in the opening:

à¤°à¤¸à¤œà¥€à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¤­à¥‚à¤¤à¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤µà¤¿à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¤‚ à¤•à¥à¤°à¥à¤¤à¥‡à¤½à¤§à¥à¤¨à¤¾à¥¥ ÅšI. 3.

It is to explain Rasa, by which KÄvya is already explained, that Ksemendra offers Aucitya. Aucitya is the very life of Rasa, the soul of poetry and this is the natural view of Aucitya in the texts of Ä€nandavardhana and bhinavagupta. In a verse or in a KÄvya, Aucitya gives CamatkÄra, Aucitya which is the life of Rasa. Rasa is the thing to which Aucitya is the greatest relation in which other things exist. He again says:

Anaucitya and HÄsya

There is one more point to be considered before closing this account of Aucitya. Bharata has said72 that HÄsya Rasa or the sentiment of laughter is produced by Anuká¹›ti and Ä€bhÄsa. It has been pointed out above that Abhinavagupta remarks in his Locana that Anaucitya is at the root of Ä€bhÄsa, as in the case of the Åšá¹›á¹…gÄrÄbhÄsa of RÄvaá¹‡a for SÄ«ta. We can only laugh at it. So it is that Laulya, which is proposed as a Rasa by some, is made by Abhinavagupta an accessory in HÄsya Rasa73.In the Abhinava bhÄratÄ« on the text of Bharata which explains the origin of HÄsya Rasa, Abhinavagupta discusses what constitutes the basis of the comic and points out that Anaucitya is at the root of the comic74 will be published”).Aucitya is Rasa and Anaucitya is RasÄbhÄsa and HÄsya Rasa. The illustrative verse quoted by Ká¹£emendra gives a series of Anaucitya and concludesâ€˜à¤¨à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤¨à¥à¤¤à¤¿ à¤•à¥‡ à¤¹à¤¾à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¤à¤¾à¤®à¥â€™. Surely one with a girdle round the neck and a necklace at the foot will be laughed at. So it is that Bharata also says:

à¤®à¥‡à¤–à¤²à¥‹à¤°à¤¸à¤¿ à¤¬à¤¨à¥à¤§à¥‡ à¤š à¤¹à¤¾à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¥ˆà¤µà¥‹à¤ªà¤œà¤¾à¤¯à¤¤à¥‡à¥¤ xxii, 69.

This takes us to another aspect of poetry and of Aucitya. In poetry of Rasa, Aucitya is the very life, JÄ«vita; but in

comic writing, the very life of its Rasa, i.e., RasÄbhÄsa or HÄsya Rasa, is Anaucitya. Anaucitya is the secret of comic writing. We can well say:

à¤šà¤¾à¤°à¥à¤µà¤¨à¥Œà¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤®à¥‡à¤µà¥ˆà¤•à¤¾ à¤¹à¤¾à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¥‹à¤ªà¤¨à¤¿à¤·à¤¤à¥à¤ªà¤°à¤¾à¥¤
à¤…à¤¨à¥Œà¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤‚ à¤°à¤¸à¤¾à¤­à¤¾à¤¸à¤•à¤¾à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤¸à¥à¤¯ à¤¸à¥à¤¥à¤¿à¤°à¤œà¥€à¤µà¤¿à¤¤à¤®à¥à¥¥

It is only with various forms of Anaucitya that HÄsya can be developed; all Doá¹£as of speech and thought occur in ÅšakÄra and we have already pointed out above how NyÅ«nopamÄand AdhikopamÄ are the secrets of satire and parody. Inappropriateness is at the root of all varieties of the ridiculous and the laughable, and this has been shown by Abhinavagupta in his Abhi. BhÄ. :

à¤…à¤¨à¥Œà¤šà¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤ªà¥à¤°à¤µà¥ƒà¤¤à¥à¤¤à¤¿à¤•à¥ƒà¤¤à¤®à¥‡à¤µ à¤¹à¤¿ à¤¹à¤¾à¤¸à¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¿à¤­à¤¾à¤µà¤¤à¥à¤µà¤®à¥à¥¤

p. 297. Gaek. edn.

Thus Anaucitya is the Aucitya in HÄsya Rasa. This Aucitya is that aspect called ‘adaptation’ by virtue of which, flaws become excellences, by change of circumstances. The incoherent and the inappropriate themselves become appropriate. Just as Åšrutidusá¹£á¹­a, a flaw in Sá¹›á¹…gÄra, is a great Guá¹‡a in Raudra and this adaptation is one Aucitya, so also Anaucitya which spoils all Rasas, and is the greatest Rasadosa, is the greatest Rasaguá¹‡a in HÄsya. This is of course said of the fundamental basis, the root cause, VibhÄva, of HÄsya Rasa and of those conditions of inappropriateness, oddities and ludicrousness which are the stuff of which HÄsya is made. And in the delineation of this Anaucitya itself producing HÄsya, in expression and in all other parts, principles of internal Aucitya have to be observed. There are two old verses on this subject of how Anaucitya becomes Aucitya,

of how Doá¹£as become Guá¹‡as and of how adaptation and appropriateness are the only rule.

(ChÄyÄof a Praká¹›t GÃ¢thÄ).

It is all some kind of relativity in the realm of poetry. There is no absolute Guá¹‡a and Doá¹£a but only Ucita and Anucita; and the poet takes up even Anaucitya to make Aucitya out of it. The poet’s attitude is as free and open in this respect as in respect of the question of morality in poetry.

It is this Aucitya which Robert Bridges speaks of in his essay on Poetic Diction under the name ‘Keeping’, a concept borrowed from Painting and which he describes as the ‘harmonising of medium ‘. The following line of his explains his idea further: â€˜But in Aesthetic no Property is absurd if it is in keeping’. Bridges speaks here of absurdity (Doá¹£a) ceasing to be so and becoming a Guá¹‡a (VaiÅ›esika) because of Aucitya (keeping).

Conclusion

Three doctrines form the great and noteworthy contributions of Sanskrit Alaá¹…kÄra Literature to the world’s literature on Literary Criticism. They are Rasa, Dhvani and Aucitya75.Aucitya is a very large principle within whose orbit comes everything else. The Aucitya-rule of criticism is obeyed by all others, including Rasa.

MahÄmahopÄdhyÄya Professor S. Kuppuswami Sastriar puts the whole evolution of Skr. Poetics from Alaá¹…kÄra to Aucitya in a KÄrikÄ and illustrates it with a graph. Within the big circle of Ká¹£emendra’s Aucitya, there are three viewpoints in the shape of a triangle. The topmost point of the triangle is the undisputed Rasa of Bharata, which Ä€nandavardhana and Abhinavagupta accept as the ‘Soul’ of poetry and which critics of Dhvani like Bhaá¹­á¹­a NÄyaka and Mahimabhaá¹­á¹­a and other theorists like Kuntaka accept. Lower down, the two points of the triangle are the two prominent theories, opposed to each other, regarding the process of realising Rasa, viz., the Dhvani of Ä€nandavardhana and the Anumiti of Mahimabhaá¹­á¹­a. Anumiti is mentioned only as ‘upalaká¹£aá¹‡a’ and it stands for other anti-dhvani theories also, like the BhÄvanÄ and Bhoga of Bhatta NÄyaka, TÄtparya etc. Within this triangle is a smaller circle named after the Vakrokti of Kuntaka. This circle again contains a triangle within it, the topmost point of which is VÄmana’s RÄ«ti, a concept decidedly supcrior to and more comprehensive than the two lower points called Guá¹‡a and Alaá¹…kÄra of Daá¹‡á¸in and BhÄmaha. Beginning with AlankÄra, the theories get superior or more comprehensive one by one. The Alaá¹…kÄra-guá¹‡a-rÄ«ti modes of criticism deal with diction and style in the lower sense of the terms and are classed under one bigger current of the study of form culminating in the comprehensive Vakrokti-circle of Kuntaka, which is also an approach to poetry from the formal side. The next, the bigger triangle begins the current of the study of the content, of the inner essence of poetry, viz., Rasa and the process, the technique by which the poet delineates it and the Sahá¹›daya gets it. All these are comprehended in the outermost circle of Aucitya which pertains to Rasa and everything else in

KÄvya. All the other theories only run at the back of Aucitya which leads the van. If there is a harmony or a beauty as such, innate in every part of a great poetry, it is this Auciti.

THE EVOLUTION OF THE NAMES OF
SANSKRIT POETICS

AND KRIYÄ€-KALPA

A PRE-BHÄ€MAHA NAME OF THE ALAá¹„KÄ€RA ÅšÄ€STRA

It will not be a surprise if on examining the history of the several names of a branch of knowledge in its long course through the centuries, one finds that it is not always the survival of the best that is the rule in the realm of nomenclatural evolution. This is borne out by an examination of the names of the subject of Sanskrit Poetics also which is called Alaá¹…kÄra ÅšÄstra, not because of the absolute appropriateness of that name. The name of the concept of Alaá¹…kÄra stuck to the whole subject even though the concept itself got dethroned after a time.

In English the subject called Literary Criticism has the old name Poetics or the Study of Poetry and we have Aristotle’s work on the subject called Poetics. In Sanskrit, the most common name for the subject and as a matter of fact, the only name which finally stood, is Alaá¹…kÄra ÅšÄstra. Sometimes we have in its place the name SÄhitya VidyÄ.â€œà¤ªà¤à¥à¤šà¤®à¥€ à¤¸à¤¾à¤¹à¤¿à¤¤à¥à¤¯à¤µà¤¿à¤¦à¥à¤¯à¤¾ à¤‡à¤¤à¤¿ à¤¯à¤¾à¤¯à¤¾à¤µà¤°à¥€à¤¯à¤ƒâ€says RÄjaÅ›ekhara. (K. M. p. 4). The name SÄhitya is very much later than the name Alaá¹…kÄra. It was evidently born out of Grammar and it slowly

came to denote poetry itself upon the basis of BhÄmaha’s definition of poetry76 :

à¤¶à¤¬à¥à¤¦à¤¾à¤°à¥à¤¥à¥Œà¤¸à¤¹à¤¿à¤¤à¥Œ à¤•à¤¾à¤µà¥à¤¯à¤®à¥à¥¤ I, 16. K. A.

SÄhitya was gaining som importance after the time of Ä€nandavardhana. It was taken up by two prominent writers who came immediately after Abhinavagupta, namely, Bhoja and Kuntaka. Sometime afterwards, we had the first regular work on Poetics which took the name SÄhitya, namely, the SÄhitya MÄ«mÄmsÄof Ruyyaka. After this, the word was in greater use and in later Alaá¹…kÄra literature one of the most important works had this name, namely, the SÄhityadarpaá¹‡a of ViÅ›vanÄtha. Whenever accomplishments of men of taste were referred to, the word SÄhitya was always used along with SÄá¹…gÄ«ta. Though not as old as Alaá¹…kÄra, SÄhitya is the only name of Sanskrit poetics, which became as common as Alaá¹…kÄra.

SÄhitya means the poetic harmony, the beautiful mutual appropriateness, the perfect mutual understanding, of Åšabda and Artha. The concept is of great significance and I have dealt with it and its history in a chapter in my book ‘Bhoja’s Åšá¹›á¹…gÄra PrakÄÅ›a. Compared with SÄhitya, the name Alaá¹…kÄra is of less poetic worth. It is a reminder of that stage in the history of Sanskrit Poetics when the concept of Alaá¹…kÄra was sitting high on the throne of poetic expression. The Alaá¹…kÄra-age of Sanskrit Poetics is much older than BhÄmaha and lived up to the time of Udbhaá¹­a, VÄmana and Rudraá¹­a. Its last great votaries were Bhoja and Kuntaka. BhÄmaha’s work is called KÄvyÄlaá¹…kÄra; Udbhaá¹­a, who commented upon BhÄmaha, names his independent work on the subject as KÄvyÄlaá¹…kÄra-sÄrasaá¹…graha; VÄmana and Rudraá¹­a only follow and name

their works also as KÄvyÄlaá¹…kÄra. Though Daá¹‡á¸in seems to be an exception, he only proves the rule; for, though he calls his work KÄvyÄdarÅ›a or Mirror of Poetry, he is the writer who pays the greatest tribute to Alaá¹…kÄra. These ancients, the Alaá¹…kÄra-vÄdins, took Alaá¹…kÄra as the beautiful expression and as the distinguishing mark of poetry, and considered even the Rasas as only subserving this beauty of expression. Bhoja ardently walks behind Daá¹‡á¸in and in his stupendous Åšá¹›á¹…gÄraprakÄÅ›a, erects a new and huge throne for Alaá¹…kÄra. Guá¹‡as Alaá¹…kÄras, RÄ«tis, Vá¹›ttis, Sandhis, Laksaá¹‡as, Rasas, Language, Metre, Form of composition, namely, epic, drama etc.,â€” why, everything is Alaá¹…kÄra to Bhoja.77The Alaá¹…kÄra-age of Sanskrit Poetics which can roughly be marked off as ending with Rudraá¹­a, is also a very significant period in the history of Sanskrit Poetics. For, it is the analysis of the Alaá¹…kÄras that led to the rise of Vakrokti and in another direction through such Alaá¹…kÄras as DÄ«paka, SamÄsokti, ParyÄyokta containing a suggested element, gave rise to the concept of suggestion, Dhvani. Vakrokti is a continuation of Alaá¹…kÄra; its greatest exponent, Kuntaka, describes his work, the Vakrokti JÄ«vita as KÄvyalaá¹…kÄra.

It is as a result of the importance of this Alaá¹…kÄra-stage of Sanskrit Poetics that the whole system got itself named after one of the several elements of poetry, Alaá¹…kÄra. Says KumÄrasvÄmin :

At the hands of VÄmana, Alaá¹…kÄra gained greater proportions; it expanded and attained greater significance and beauty. It came to him from Daá¹‡á¸in and when he turned that stone of Alaá¹…kÄra handed to him, he found it flashing diverse hues. He realized that it meant Beauty. It is this Beauty that is otherwise called CamatkÄra on which word ViÅ›veÅ›vara, the author of the CamatkÄracandrikÄ, takes his stand. The words Vicchitti, Vaicitrya, and even the word VakratÄ finally mean only Beauty. It is the same, the beautiful in poetry, that is meant by the Ramaá¹‡Ä«ya in JagannÄtha’s definition of poetry. From this point of view, it seems that there was good chance for a new name for Poetics, namely Saundarya ÅšÄstra, but it did not come up. The name Saundarya ÅšÄstra would correspond to the western name Aesthetics. In the western literature on the subject, the words, the Beautiful and the Sublime, are met with. There are the works such as that of Longinus on the Sublime. One whole chapter, in his work, ‘What is Art ?’, is devoted by Tolstoy to an examination of the works on Beauty. But the study of Beauty and Sublimity, Aesthetics or Saundarya ÅšÄstra, does not strictly mean Poetics but embraces the critical appreciation of all Fine Arts, including sculpture, painting and music.

In Uddyota I and elsewhere, Ä€nandavardhana refers to writers on Poetics as KÄvya-laká¹£aá¹‡a-kÄrÄ«ns, for, those who wrote on poetry did so with the idea of defining Poetry. (Dhva. Ä€. pp. 8, 10, etc.) And KÄvya-laká¹£aá¹‡a can also be taken as a general appellation applied to Poetics in the days of the reign of Alaá¹…kÄra and even earlier. CAMATKÄ€RA

At first, works on Poetics approached from the stand-point of Alaá¹…kÄra and were invariably named also KÄvyÄlaá¹…kÄra. Then, with the rise of Rasa and Dhvani, works on Poetics approached the subject from the ‘Ä€tman’ of poetry, namely Rasa-Dhvani. Then came Bhoja, whose work, the Åšá¹›á¹…gÄra prakÄÅ›a, among the many points which it emphasised, emphasised the concept of SÄhitya also, which together with the brilliant exposition of that concept in Kuntaka’s Vakrokti JÄ«vita, gave rise to a new kind of aproach for a Poetics-treatise in the works called SÄhitya mÄ«mÄmsÄ.^(1) Another approach is that of CamatkÄra, the literary delight which comprehends all the poetical elements from Guá¹‡a and ÅšabdÄlaá¹…kÄra to Rasa and Dhvani. It is clear that when we read poetry, we have a certain enjoyment; this enjoyment may be due in one place to a sound effect, to a striking idea in another, and to the emotional movement in still another; but it is all the same one relish.

It is a striking coincidence that, like the concept of Rasa, the concept of CamatkÄra also came into the Alaá¹…kÄra ÅšÄstra from the PÄka Å›Ästra. Its early semantic history is indistinct and dictionaries record only the later meanings, the chief of

which are â€˜astonishmentâ€™and â€˜poetic relishâ€™. In appears to me that originally the word CamatkÄra was an onomatopoeic word referring to the clicking sound we make with our tongue when we taste something snappy, and in the course of its semantic enlargements, CamatkÄra came to mean a sudden fillip relating to any feeling of a pleasurable type. NÄrÄ-yaá¹‡a, an ancestor of the author of the SÄhitya darpana, interpreted CamatkÄra as an expansion of the heart, Citta vistÄra, and held all kinds of Rasa-realisation to be of the nature of this CamatkÄra or Citta vistÃ£ra, of which the best example was the Adbhuta rasa. But as a general and all comprehensive name for literary relish, the word CamatkÄra occurs even in the DhvanyÄloka (p. 144, N. S. edn.). In the same sense, the word occurs about fourteen times in the Locana of Abhinavagupta (pp. 37, 63, 65, 69, 72, 79, 113, 137 and 138). From the reference on p. 63 we understand that Bhaá¹­á¹­a NÄyaka also used the word in the same sense. On p. 65, Abhinavagupta describes Rasa to be of the nature of CamatkÄra. Kuntaka uses the word in the same sense. The Agni purÄá¹‡a equates the Caitanya of the Ä€tman, CamatkÄra and Rasa.(Ch. 339, S’I. 2).

Abhinavagupta’s pupil Ká¹£emendra, whose brain went on many a refreshing and original line, made an approach to poetry through this CamatkÄra in one of his small but interesting works, the Kavikaná¹­hÄbharaá¹‡a. The third Sandhi of this work is called CamatkÄra kathana and here, Ká¹£emendra analyses the points of CamatkÄra in a poem into ten.
